601 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Slotcavage


No. 545 MDA 2007


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 378; 939 A.2d 901; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4141


September 17, 2007, Submitted 
December 13, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected February 13, 2008


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence February 26, 2007. In the Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County. Criminal at No(s): CP-54-CR-0000567-2005. Before DOLBIN, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County (Pennsylvania) granted the Commonwealth's motion for reconsideration after defendant was sentenced to one to two years of electronic monitoring. It then found that 18 Pa.C.S. § 7508, under which defendant was sentenced for his conviction of felony delivery of a controlled substance, set forth a mandatory minimum sentence of one year in prison. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of delivery of a controlled substance, in violation of 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 780-113, which was a felony. The substance was 4.8 grams of cocaine. The trial court initially sentenced defendant to one to two years' electronic monitoring, but subsequently granted the Commonwealth's motion for reconsideration, which claimed that 18 Pa.C.S. § 7508 set forth a mandatory minimum sentence of one year in prison for felony delivery under 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 780-113(a)(30). Defendant argued on appeal that the trial court erred when it modified his first sentence because, inter alia, application of the mandatory minimum sentence was unconstitutional under Booker. The appellate court disagreed and found that the trial court did not err in finding that 18 Pa.C.S. § 7508 required at least one year in prison. The appellate court so concluded because it found that the 18 Pa.C.S. § 7508 did not violate Apprendi, Blakely, Booker, or defendant's Sixth Amendment rights. The statute dealt with mandatory minimum sentences and not the constitutional problem of a trial court increasing a sentence beyond the statutory maximum based on facts not submitted to a jury.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of sentence.

 

602 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Henderson


No. 1582 Western District Appeal 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 374; 938 A.2d 1063; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4137


June 12, 2007, Argued 
December 11, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended February 7, 2008.
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Henderson, 598 Pa. 746, 954 A.2d 575, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1226 (2008)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Henderson, 2016 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1651 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, No. 306 of 2006. Before DiSANTIS, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), following his plea of guilty to robbery, aggravated assault, recklessly endangering another person, and possession of instruments of a crime.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court noted that defendant received a 25 to 50-year mandatory sentence for robbery as a "third strike," and then another 25 to 50-year sentence enhancement for aggravated assault as a "fourth strike." Both charges arose from the same incident, and defendant was convicted and sentenced for both on the same dates. Applying the sentence enhancement for both robbery and aggravated assault was in error. The sentence for aggravated assault was vacated. It did not matter that defendant's sentences were concurrent. Defendant had to be sentenced for a "third strike" and given the opportunity to reform before he could be convicted and sentenced for a "fourth strike." Since defendant's sentences were concurrent, the overall sentencing scheme was not compromised, and it was not necessary to remand for re-sentencing. Finally, defendant's 2001 robbery conviction counted for purposes of 42 Pa.C.S. § 9714(g), even though it was in federal, rather than state, court. Section 9714(a)(2) was unambiguous. Proof that defendant received notice of the penalties under § 9714(a)(1) was not required.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated insofar as it related to the aggravated assault conviction, and was affirmed in all other respects.

 

603 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Commonwealth v. Habay


No. 1518 WDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 303; 934 A.2d 732; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3125


July 2, 2007, Submitted 
October 10, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Habay, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1056 (Pa., July 2, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, No. CP-02-CR-0015806-2004. Before MANNING, J.
Commonwealth v. Habay, 2006 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 482 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) convicted defendant, a member of the state legislature, of conflict of interest under 65 Pa.C.S. § 1103(a). It acquitted him of theft of services under 18 Pa.C.S. § 3926(b). Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the conflict of interest charge should have been dismissed under 1 Pa.C.S. § 1933 because 65 Pa.C.S. § 1103 was a general statute and the theft of services statute was a specific one. The court stated that although both statutes prohibited the wrongful diversion of services for pecuniary gain, § 1103 was limited to public officials acting through the authority of their offices. Thus, defendant was convicted under the more specific provision. Next, the evidence supported the conviction. It demonstrated that defendant in his official capacity directed several state employees to conduct political work, such as fund-raising efforts, for him at times when the employees were being paid by taxpayers to perform work for constituents. Defendant derived the benefit of a substantial number of hours of labor for which he otherwise would have needed to pay. Section 1103 was not vague under the state or federal constitutions on its face or as applied to defendant. There was nothing unclear about the concept of using the authority of an office to obtain private pecuniary benefit. Finally, the statute was not overbroad. Nothing in it reached protected behavior.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.


 

604 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Morrison


No. 352 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 294; 934 A.2d 709; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3113


August 22, 2005, Submitted 
September 27, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division, No. CP-67-CR-0002676-2004. Before BLACKWELL, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial in the York County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol (DUI) under 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802. He appealed, arguing that § 3802(c) violated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 because it was vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court had rejected a claim that § 3802(a)(2) was unconstitutionally vague because it was unclear when the crime occurred. The supreme court had emphasized that § 3802 proscribed the act of driving after drinking a sufficient amount of alcohol, where a sufficient amount of alcohol was that quantity that would cause a person's blood alcohol content (BAC) level to reach the statutorily prohibited range within two hours after driving, regardless of the actor's BAC level at the actual time of driving. Although § 3802(a)(2) applied to BAC between 0.08 and 0.10 percent within two hours after driving, and § 3802(c) referred to BAC of 0.16 percent or higher, the language of the subsections was otherwise the same. Accordingly, § 3802(c) was not unconstitutionally vague because it did not specify that a person's BAC be above 0.16 percent at the time he was driving and because it did not specify when a person could resume driving. The prohibited conduct was drinking excessively and then driving. Next, the supreme court had specifically held that § 3802(c) was not overbroad; thus, this claim failed too.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.

 

605 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Taylor


No. 369 WDA 2007


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 282; 933 A.2d 1035; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3049


July 16, 2007, Submitted 
September 7, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Taylor, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3859 (Pa. Super. Ct., Nov. 7, 2007)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Taylor, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 992 (Pa., June 26, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the PCRA Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, No. CC 199600105. Before DURKIN, J.
Commonwealth v. Taylor, 737 A.2d 813, 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1232 (Pa. Super. Ct., 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner sought review of an order entered in the Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) which dismissed his petition brought pursuant to the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546. The issue was whether the prisoner timely filed his current PCRA petition, and if not whether there was an exception.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner filed his first PCRA petition and the PCRA court appointed counsel. Counsel filed a "no-merit" letter. The PCRA court granted counsel permission to withdraw. It subsequently dismissed the petition pursuant Pa. R. Crim. P. 907. The prisoner filed his current PCRA petition over five years after his judgment of sentence became final. Thus, his current petition was untimely. Nevertheless, the prisoner sought to invoke the exception to the PCRA time bar under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9545(b)(1)(ii). The prisoner insisted that he could not have reasonably known within the time allotted following direct review that the law barred the coroner from signing his arrest warrant. The appellate court held the prisoner bore the burden of proving why he did not file a timely PCRA petition. His warrant was a matter of public record and was accessible to him. Although he asserted he could not have known of his claim until later, he could have reasonably discovered the coroner's signature on the warrant long before he filed his PCRA petition. His claim did not qualify as an exception to the PCRA timeliness requirements and PCRA counsel could not be deemed ineffective for having failed to pursue it.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Robinson


No. 3361 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 230; 931 A.2d 15; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2570


October 26, 2006, Argued 
August 2, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 6, 2008


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No. 02-12-0272. Before HUGHES, J.
Commonwealth v. Robinson, 859 A.2d 835, 2004 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3432 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of multiple counts of corruption of a minor and indecent assault (18 Pa.C.S. §§ 6301 and 3126, respectively. The Philadelphia County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), denied his postconviction motions but granted the Commonwealth's motion to increase his sentence. After remand, it re-sentenced defendant to an aggregate prison term of three to six years, plus five years' probation. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged the trial court vindictively increased his sentence because he filed post-sentence motions. The appellate court held that this claim was waived because it implicated the discretionary aspects of the sentence, not its legality; it was not included in his Pa.R.A.P. 2119(f) statement; and the Commonwealth objected. It disapproved Commonwealth v. Walker, 568 A.2d 201 (Pa. Super. 1989); Commonwealth v. Maly, 558 A.2d 877 (Pa. Super. 1989); Commonwealth v. Mikesell, 537 A.2d 1372 (Pa. Super. 1988); Commonwealth v. Johnson, 860 A.2d 146 (Pa. Super. 2004); and Commonwealth v. McHale, 2007 PA Super. 131, to the extent that they suggested a claim of vindictiveness was a non-waivable challenge to a sentence's legality. Though the trial court improperly warned defendant not to file a motion for sentence reconsideration, there was no indication it acted vindictively. As fondling the victim's breast was corruption of a minor, and touching her vagina was the separate crime of indecent assault, the two crimes did not merge. The trial court should have given reasons for sentencing defendant outside of the guidelines, but it articulated adequate reasons for the sentence as a whole.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Perreault


No. 1612 MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 214; 930 A.2d 553; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2178


April 30, 2007, Submitted 
July 17, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Perreault, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 246 (Pa., Mar. 20, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Criminal Division, No(s): CP-36-CR-0002056-1998. Before ASHWORTH, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted, upon a guilty plea, of interception, disclosure or use of wire, electronic, or oral communications, and harassment. Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) following the revocation of defendant's probation. Defendant furnished a concise statement of matters complained of on appeal. The trial court submitted its Pa.R.A.P. 1925(a) opinion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence that he had violated a probation condition. The appellate court held that defendant admitted to a polygraph examiner and a probation officer that he had twice watched X-rated movies. The fact that defendant masturbated to one of the movies and destroyed it further supported the conclusion that the movies depicted prohibited sexual conduct. The condition's restriction on viewing or possessing nude depictions was neither vague nor overbroad. It did not impose a ban against "mere nudity" as found in works of serious art. The condition incorporated the nudity restriction within the larger prohibition against possessing obscene materials, or materials which depicted sexual conduct. The generally accepted meaning of the terms "obscene," or "sexual conduct" could be found by referring to 18 Pa.C.S. § 5903(b). The condition's terms were specific and well-recognized in the context of law enforcement against sex offenses, did not sweep overbroadly into constitutionally protected conduct, only applied to probationers, and directly related to a legitimate interest in reducing the recidivism of sex offender probationers.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Snead v. SPCA


No. 3313 Eastern District Appeal 2005, No. 355 Eastern District Appeal 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 204; 929 A.2d 1169; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2079


October 3, 2006, Argued 
July 11, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Snead v. SPCA, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3093 (Pa. Super. Ct., Sept. 13, 2007)
Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Snead v. SPCA, 600 Pa. 372, 966 A.2d 548, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 242 (2009)
Affirmed by Snead v. SPCA of Pa., 985 A.2d 909, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2786 (Pa., 2009)
Appeal denied by Snead v. SPCA of Pa., 2010 Pa. LEXIS 672 (Pa., Apr. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order and Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, No. 002921 March Term, 2001. Before RUSSELL, J.
Snead v. SPCA, 2006 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 75 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff dog owner brought suit against defendant animal welfare organization after it euthanized her dogs. A jury in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) found in favor of plaintiff on her conversion, negligence, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims. It awarded compensatory and punitive damages. The trial court denied plaintiff's motion for counsel fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. Defendant appealed. Plaintiff cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff's dogs were removed from her home and plaintiff charged with dog-fighting. After the charges were dropped, plaintiff was mistakenly told that the dogs had been euthanized. They were actually euthanized several days later. The court rejected defendant's argument that the conversion and negligence claims were barred by sovereign immunity and by 42 Pa.C.S. § 8541. Defendant was a private corporation, not a Commonwealth agency under 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 8501 and 102. It elected its own directors and officers, and the Commonwealth did not control its operations. There was sufficient evidence to support the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim. A jury could find the killing of the dogs to be unreasonable under the Fourth Amendment. As for due process, plaintiff had a property interest in her dogs, and there was sufficient evidence that defendant had inadequate procedures in place to safeguard that interest. Defendant's conduct did not support the punitive damages award. There was no evidence that it acted in conscious disregard of a risk of harm to plaintiff. Finally, plaintiff was entitled to counsel fees, as she had established a constitutional violation and received compensatory damages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of punitive damages and the denial of counsel fees. It affirmed the rest of the judgment. It remanded the case for entry of judgment on compensatory damages and counsel fees.

Commonwealth v. Nischan


No. 821 MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 199; 928 A.2d 349; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2030


August 28, 2006, Submitted 
July 5, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Revised August 24, 2007.
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Nischan, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2427 (Pa., Nov. 20, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of April 10, 2006, in the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Criminal Division, No. CP-06-CR-0000148-2006. Before LUDGATE, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence by the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania). Defendant's attorney filed a petition to withdraw as counsel alleging that the appeal was frivolous. Counsel also filed a brief pursuant to Anders presenting issues that might arguably support the appeal, which included whether the trial court erred by imposing a mandatory minimum sentence of ten years pursuant to 42 Pa.C.S. § 9714(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to burglary. The crime occurred in a home while a person was present, making the offense a crime of violence under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9714(g). In an earlier case, defendant was convicted of a burglary that also qualified as a crime of violence. As such, the trial court sentenced him under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9714(a) which imposed a mandatory minimum term of not less than ten nor more than twenty years for the subject burglary. The petition averred that counsel reviewed the record and relevant legal authority and indicated counsel's conclusion that there were no non-frivolous issues to be raised on appeal. The petition specifically stated that, following counsel's review, he believed the appeal was wholly frivolous. The appellate court held counsel had met the three requirements of Anders. Because the burglary was a crime of violence, the second strike penalty applied to defendant. The trial court imposed the mandatory second strike sentence as required by law. Accordingly, that issue was frivolous. His claim that he did not know of the mandatory sentence at the time of the plea was frivolous.


OUTCOME: The petition to withdraw as counsel was granted. The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

 

610 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


In the Interest of T.E.H.


NO. 325, MDA 2006, NO. 326, MDA 2006, NO. 327, MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 193; 928 A.2d 318; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1722


August 14, 2006, Submitted 
June 28, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Revised August 23, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order Entered January 12, 2006. In Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division, No(s). 364 JADL 2003; 876 JV 2005, 466 JV 2005, 148 JADL 2005; 211 JV 2005. Before UHLER, J.

Appeal from the Order Entered January 12, 2006. In the Court of Common Pleas of YORK County. CRIMINAL at No(s): 466 JV 2005.

Appeal from the Order Entered January 12, 2006. In the Court of Common Pleas of YORK County. CRIMINAL at No(s): 148 JADL 2005, 211 JV 2005.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, three juveniles, in consolidated appeals from the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), challenged the validity of the DNA Act, 44 Pa.C.S. § 2316. Each juvenile had been ordered to submit a DNA sample and each had filed a motion to quash, which had been denied.


OVERVIEW: The juveniles argued that the trial court's application of the Act retroactively appended a condition on fully-served dispositions. Two had been discharged from formal probation pursuant to felony adjudications prior to the effective date of the most recent Act amendment. The third, though, was still subject to a dispositional order entered pursuant to his felony adjudication. In each case, the trial court ordered DNA sampling pursuant to later misdemeanor and summary offenses. One juvenile was no longer under supervision, however, for his prior felony adjudication; the only reason that he was confined was his subsequent misdemeanor adjudications. Thus, the Act did not apply to him. On the other hand, the third juvenile remained under supervision for his felony adjudications until after the amendment, and so he was properly ordered to provide a sample. As to the second, though the Commonwealth contended that he was not "technically" subject to the Act as his supervision for a felony adjudication ended five days before the amendment, and that he should not benefit, such was jurisdictional. In addition, the Act was neither vague nor did it facilitate unreasonable search and seizure.


OUTCOME: The superior court held that two of the juveniles were not subject to the dictates of the DNA Act, and it, therefore, reversed the orders directing them to submit to DNA sampling. In contrast, it concluded that the third juvenile was subject to the provisions of the DNA Act. Furthermore, the DNA Act was not void due to vagueness, nor did it violate the third juvenile's right to privacy or right to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures.

 

611 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Harley


No. 759 MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 148; 924 A.2d 1273; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1237


January 2, 2007, Submitted  
May 25, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Commonwealth v. Harley, 594 Pa. 35, 934 A.2d 1149, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2115 (2007)
Appeal dismissed by Commonwealth v. Harley, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2284 (Pa., Dec. 17, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Criminal Division, No. CP-06-CR-0004544-2005. Before SPRECHER, J.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Berks County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), imposed following his convictions of possession of a controlled substance and possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance, in violation of 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 780-113(a)(16), (30). Specifically, defendant was sentenced to five to 10 years' imprisonment under 18 Pa.C.S. § 7508(a)(2)(ii).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that he possessed less than 10 grams of crack cocaine with an intent to deliver because he planned to use a portion of the cocaine. Alternatively, he argued that the procedure employed by the Commonwealth in weighing the drugs was flawed as it did not account for the variances in weight in the ziplock bags containing the cocaine. The appellate court concluded that the evidence was sufficient to demonstrate that defendant intended to distribute all 10.2 grams of the cocaine. The fact that the 87 baggies were in a larger plastic bag indicated defendant was preparing to distribute the drugs and the lack of use paraphernalia and the $ 480 on defendant's person indicated an intent to deliver. In addition, the extrapolation conducted by the Commonwealth accurately determined the weight of the drugs found on defendant because averaging 14 empty identical bags or 15% of the total bags accurately demonstrated the average weight of one bag and accounted for any variances between the bags. 18 Pa.C.S. § 7508 did not violate equal protection rights because it was rationally related to the legislature's goal in fighting the spread of drugs in communities.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

 

612 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


In re S.A.


No. 967 EDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 137; 925 A.2d 838; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1184


November 14, 2006, Argued  
May 17, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by In re S.A., 2008 Pa. LEXIS 858 (Pa., June 3, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Wayne County,. Civil Division, No.: 28-Civil-2006. CP-64-JV-0020044-2000. Before CONWAY, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, then a juvenile, was adjudicated a delinquent and placed in a sex offender treatment program. Later, the Court of Common Pleas of Wayne County (Pennsylvania) ordered his commitment under the Involuntary Treatment of Certain Sexually Violent Persons Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 6401-6409. Appellant challenged the trial court's decision. He argued in part that the Act was retroactive in violation of 1 Pa.C.S. § 1926 and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the Act was not retroactive in effect. It did not relate to a juvenile's prior offenses, but to his current status. The Act was not punitive and thus did not require proof beyond a reasonable doubt. Its purpose was to provide necessary treatment and to protect the public, and it had a nonpunitive effect. As the Act was nonpunitive, it did not violate ex post facto principles. Next, the alleged inconvenience of disclosing information to mental health professionals in an attempt to effectuate a determination under the Act was greatly outweighed by the interest in treating juveniles and ensuring public safety. Disclosure under the Act did not violate 42 Pa.C.S. § 6307, which stated that court files and records were open to inspection by the State Sexual Offenders Assessment Board. The Act did not violate equal protection. Although a right to physical freedom was fundamental, the Commonwealth had a compelling interest in protecting the public from danger. The Act, which provided for annual review as a means to guard against excessive commitment, was narrowly tailored to effectuate this interest. Finally, 42 Pa.C.S. § 6403(d) was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

 

613 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Matroni


No. 750 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 110; 923 A.2d 444; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 770


October 10, 2006, Submitted  
April 19, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Matroni, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 691 (Pa., May 15, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Criminal Division, No(s): 2432 of 2004. Before PARISI, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a jury trial in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), defendant was found guilty of homicide by vehicle under 75 Pa.C.S. § 3732, as well as the summary offenses of overtaking a vehicle on the right, reckless driving, following too closely, driving on roadways laned for traffic, and driving at a safe speed. He was sentenced to 8 to 23 months' imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant raised several issues. The appellate court found sufficient evidence that defendant was guilty of homicide by vehicle beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant's course of conduct immediately prior to the accident violated five separate provisions of the Pennsylvania Vehicle Code. Defendant was speeding, tailgating, and erratically changing lanes, which was sufficient to show that he acted in a reckless and grossly negligent manner. Defendant's actions evidenced a conscious disregard of the substantial and unjustified risk that he would be involved in a traffic accident causing death. In addition, the trial court erred in instructing the jury on the sudden emergency doctrine because that doctrine, a civil concept, was not a recognized defense to which defendant was entitled to a jury instruction. Because this error had the potential to prejudice the Commonwealth, not defendant, defendant was not entitled to relief on his claim that the instruction was incorrect. Also, defendant could not avail himself of the doctrine because he was the cause of the sudden emergency. Finally, defendant did not show error on the part of the trial court in fashioning his sentence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction and judgment of sentence.

 

614 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Fulton


No. 296 WDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 97; 921 A.2d 1239; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 720


September 19, 2006, Argued  
April 9, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Fulton, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2116 (Pa., Oct. 4, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Criminal Division, No.: 549 Criminal 2004. Before CASCIO, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of violating 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(a)(1) and (c), § 1543(b)(1.1), and § 3309(1), and sentenced him to one to five years' imprisonment for the driving under the influence (DUI) conviction, and a concurrent 90 days' imprisonment for the driving with a suspended license-DUI related conviction.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant challenged the denial of his suppression motion. The appellate court found that the trooper, a policeman with more than five years' experience, saw defendant swerve out of his lane of travel three times in a mere 30 seconds, in dense fog, on a road shared by oncoming traffic. Under such circumstances, the trooper possessed reasonable suspicion that defendant was violating a provision of the Motor Vehicle Code. Thus, the stop was legal and the trial court correctly denied the motion to suppress. Defendant also argued that he was erroneously sentenced as a four-time, instead of a three-time, DUI offender because when he pled guilty and entered the accelerated rehabilitative disposition (ARD) program following his first DUI, 75 Pa.C.S. § 3806 stated that the guilty plea and entry into the ARD program could only be used in calculating his repeat offender status for seven years. The appellate court concluded that this argument had been addressed by the Tustin case. Thus, there was no error in the sentence imposed upon defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

 

615 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Williams


No. 96 MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 73; 920 A.2d 887; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 321


January 9, 2007, Submitted  
March 14, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal Division, No(s). 1399 of 2005. Before OLSZEWSKI, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to possession with intent to deliver heroin, firearms possessed by a felon, and carrying a firearm without a license. The Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), sentenced him to 5 to 10 years for the drug charge, a consecutive term of two to four years for firearms possessed by a felon, and a consecutive term of one to two years for carrying a firearm without a license. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that he was illegally sentenced twice for the same criminal act of possessing a firearm without a license. In Pennsylvania, the Jones case provided a lead opinion approach to merger and a dissenting opinion approach to merger. The appellate court adopted the dissenting approach for two reasons. First, the dissenting approach reflected and gave proper deference to 42 Pa.C.S. § 9765, a statute that had not been ruled unconstitutional by the Pennsylvania Supreme Court. Second, the dissenting approach more accurately reflected the appellate court's jurisprudence on merger. Thus, the appellate court held that the trial court did not err as a matter of law by imposing separate sentences for the weapons offenses. 18 Pa.C.S. § 6105(a) contained a statutory element that 18 Pa.C.S. § 6106(a) did not: namely, conviction of an enumerated offense. Under 18 Pa.C.S. § 6105, the Commonwealth did not need to prove that defendant lacked a valid license. Rather, it only had to prove that defendant was convicted of an enumerated offense. Similarly, 18 Pa.C.S. § 6106(a) contained a statutory element that 18 Pa.C.S. § 6105(a) did not: namely, lack of a valid license.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Kerrigan


No. 3257 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 63; 920 A.2d 190; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 307


October 18, 2006, Argued  
March 8, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Kerrigan, 594 Pa. 676, 932 A.2d 1286, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2061 (2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Transferred by Kerrigan v. Lamas, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 89954 (M.D. Pa., Aug. 12, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Criminal Division, No. 2183/2003. Before STEINBERG, J.
Commonwealth v. Kerrigan, 2005 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 519 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of rape of a child, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse with a child, aggravated indecent assault, indecent assault, and corruption of minors. The Court of Common Pleas, Lehigh County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) sentenced defendant to 25 to 50 years' imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: In response to defendant's many assignments of error, the superior court concluded, inter alia, that: (1) the victim's testimony was sufficient to allow the jury to find that defendant penetrated the victim with his penis in Pennsylvania; (2) counsel was not ineffective for failing to object to the trial court's instructions on the grounds that they shifted the burden of believability to defendant as the jury instructions provided the jury with the general hornbook law regarding the weight and credibility it should give the testimony; (3) a single stray remark by a witness indicating that defendant had been in prison did not necessitate a mistrial; (4) counsel was not ineffective for failing to request a cautionary instruction as to defendant's prior criminal acts as the evidence established that he sexually abused the victim over three years and failed to show how the failure caused prejudice; and (5) the transmission of Human Papillomavirus and genital warts satisfied the serious bodily injury requirements for the rape and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse convictions because of their permanent nature and the risk of passing the virus to future sex partners or children.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Rabold


NO. 832 EDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 62; 920 A.2d 857; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 309


November 14, 2006, Submitted  
March 8, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised March 15, 2007.
Appeal granted by Commonwealth v. Rabold, 594 Pa. 310, 935 A.2d 1270, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2341 (2007)
Affirmed by Commonwealth v. Rabold, 597 Pa. 344, 951 A.2d 329, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1178 (2008)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Rabold v. Patti-Worthington, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 47446 (M.D. Pa., Apr. 8, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Criminal Division, No(s) CP-45-CR-0000389-2003. Before WORTHINGTON, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was found guilty but mentally ill of attempted homicide and aggravated assault (18 Pa.C.S. §§ 314, 901, 2501, 2702), and guilty of possession of an instrument of crime (18 Pa.C.S. § 907). The Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania) sentenced him to 14 to 28 years on the attempted homicide conviction and to not more than one year for the possession of an instrument of crime conviction. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, appellant raised several issues. The appellate court concluded that the evidence was sufficient to sustain appellant's convictions because the guilty but mentally ill convictions did not extinguish the requisite mens rea to impose criminal liability for criminal homicide and aggravated assault. In addition, the jury was free to resolve the conflict in the expert testimony in the Commonwealth's favor. Also, there was no abuse of discretion by the trial court in rejecting appellant's claim that the verdict was against the weight of the evidence because this claim was predicated on the conflicting expert testimony, and the jury was free to believe the Commonwealth's expert. Finally, appellant argued that the jury instruction impermissibly allowed the jury to convict on a standard short of proof beyond a reasonable doubt. However, appellant conceded that this very argument had been rejected by the appellate court. The trial court instructed the jury on the concepts of legal insanity and guilty but mentally ill using a suggested standard jury instruction that had been specifically approved by the appellate court, and correctly set forth the state of the law in Pennsylvania.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Etchison


No. 55 WDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 26; 916 A.2d 1169; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 101


June 26, 2006, Submitted  
January 24, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Commonwealth v. Etchison, 935 A.2d 1267, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2343 (Pa., 2007)
Affirmed by Commonwealth v. Etchison, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 263 (Pa., Mar. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Criminal Division, No. C.A. 2739 of 2004. Before HANCHER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence imposed by the Butler County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) following his conviction at a bench trial of driving under the influence in violation of 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(1)(i), (2) and (3), and several summary offenses.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed that the evidence was insufficient to support defendant's conviction under 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(2) and (3). The Commonwealth's expert witness testified that the presence of metabolites was not an indication of present impairment but only that a substance was ingested sometime previously. The Commonwealth presented no evidence to support a conclusion that defendant was under the influence of a drug or combination of drugs and alcohol at the time he was stopped, such that his ability to drive was impaired. However, defendant's overbreadth challenge to 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(1) failed because there was no constitutional right to the use of marijuana prior to driving. Defendant's equal protection challenge to 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(1) also failed because there was no legislative classification upon which to base such an argument. 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(1) prohibited the operation of a motor vehicle by any driver who had any amount of specifically enumerated controlled substances in his blood, regardless of impairment. All drivers were treated the same.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and judgment of sentence under 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(1)(i), and reversed defendant's convictions under 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(d)(2) and (3) and discharged him as to those charges. The court remanded for resentencing.

Commonwealth v. Smith


No. 1885 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 25; 917 A.2d 848; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 99


June 12, 2006, Submitted  
January 23, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal Division, No. CP 14 CR 511 - 2005. Before KISTLER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted and sentenced him for violating 75 Pa.C.S. §§ 3802(a)(1) and (b), 6503.1, 1543(B)(1.1)(ii), and 1501(a), relating to driving under the influence of alcohol, habitual offenders, driving while operating privilege was suspended or revoked, and driving without a valid license.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant raised numerous issues. The appellate court concluded that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress because an officer was not required to have probable cause to effectuate a valid stop where the probable cause standard had been superseded by 75 Pa.C.S. § 6308(b). The officer was only required to possess reasonable suspicion. Reasonable suspicion was created as the result of a citizen's 911 call, and the officer's own observations of defendant driving on the wrong side of the road, and accelerating away from the police cruiser when the officer activated its overhead lights. Defendant's arguments raising constitutional challenges to the statutes under which he was convicted were rejected by the appellate court because the exact same arguments had been previously raised and rejected in a different case. Because one of defendant's arguments was not contained in his court ordered Pa.R.A.P. 1925(b) statement of matters complained of on appeal, it was waived. Finally, the trial court properly concluded that the failure to produce a videotape affected the weight of the officer's testimony, not the admissibility of that testimony.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

In the Interest of K.A.P.


No. 769 WDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 22; 916 A.2d 1152; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 50


October 24, 2006, Argued  
January 19, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by In the Interest of K.A.P., 934 A.2d 686, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2064 (Pa., 2007)
Affirmed by In the Interest of K.A.P., 2008 Pa. LEXIS 262 (Pa., Mar. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, No. 80743 of 2002. Before TRUCILLA, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to chapter 64 of the Juvenile Code, 42 Pa.C.S. § 6401 et seq., the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), committed appellant to one year of involuntary civil commitment for juvenile sexual offenders. Appellant challenged the commitment order.


OVERVIEW: Under the plain language of 42 Pa.C.S. § 6403(a), appellant was eligible for involuntary treatment even though he was in state prison rather than a juvenile facility on his 20th birthday. Although the literal language of the statute supported appellant's interpretation, it lead to an absurd result as his criminal actions while in a juvenile facility placed him in state prison. Thus, appellant's interpretation would have rendered 42 Pa.C.S. § 6401 et seq., void by the intentional and criminal actions of the very people the law was intended to benefit. The claim that 42 Pa.C.S. § 6403(d) was unconstitutionally void for vagueness failed as the phrase "serious difficulty in controlling sexually violent behavior" was no more vague than the words and phrases challenged in cases upholding Megan's Law II, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9791 et seq. 42 Pa.C.S. § 6401 et seq., did not apply retroactively as it did not relate to a juvenile's prior offense; it only related to a juvenile's current and continuing status as a person who suffered a mental abnormality that resulted in serious difficulty controlling sexually violent behavior. The equal protection and cruel and unusual punishment claims also failed.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Beshore


No. 633 MDA 2005, No. 634 MDA 2005, No. 696 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 19; 916 A.2d 1128; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 47


October 26, 2006, Argued  
January 18, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. Neufeld, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2234 (Pa., Oct. 22, 2009)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Beshore, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2235 (Pa., Oct. 22, 2009)
Appeal denied by, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. Imes, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2250 (Pa., Oct. 22, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County and Cumberland County, Criminal Division, Nos. 3413 CR 2004, 2483 CR 2004 and CP-21 CR 1393-2004. Before KLEINFELTER and BAYLEY, JJ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In three separate cases tried in the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) and in the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), defendants were convicted of driving under the influence in violation of 75 Pa.C.S. §§ 3801-3817. Defendants, who were represented by the same attorney, appealed. They raised identical issues challenging the constitutionality of the statutes.


OVERVIEW: The court held that 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802 was not void for vagueness or overbroad because it did not contain a "time nexus" that would link the act of drinking with the subsequent act of driving. It gave a person of ordinary intelligence notice that he could not drive after imbibing a sufficient amount of alcohol to prevent him from driving safely. Furthermore, § 3802 provided a reasonable standard for drivers to gauge their conduct. It was not overbroad because it punished drivers who might not have reached the prohibited blood alcohol level at the time of driving, but reached it later. The court had previously held that there was nothing in § 3802 that arguably permitted a person to drive with a blood alcohol content (BAC) below a certain level. Next, the court had previously rejected an argument that § 3802 was unconstitutional because police officers could discriminatorily or arbitrarily time the BAC test to ensnare drunk drivers. Finally, two defendants lacked standing to challenge the penal provision, 75 Pa.C.S. § 3804(b), because there had not been an accident involved. The third had waived the issue under Pa.R.A.P. 2119 by not arguing as to how he was affected by the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of sentence.

Commonwealth v. Hernandez


No. 33 WDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 15; 917 A.2d 332; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 21


October 30, 2006, Submitted  
January 12, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Revised March 8, 2007. As Revised March 9, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Entered August 26, 2005, Court of Common Pleas, Venango County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 910-2004 & 1036-2004.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant "guilty but mentally ill" and convicted him of criminal trespass and simple assault. The Venango County, Pennsylvania, Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, denied his post-sentence motion to modify costs and charged him with the costs of the prosecution's mental health expert. He appealed, arguing that 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1403, which required a convicted defendant to pay the costs of prosecution, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that § 1403 violated Fuller v. Oregon and was inadequate to protect his due process and equal protection rights because it did not require the trial court, before ordering him to pay the costs of prosecution, to take into account whether he had the ability to pay those costs. The appellate court disagreed. Fuller mandated only that § 1403 contain a procedural device that ensured that a defendant would not be imprisoned if he was financially unable to pay the costs of prosecution. Although § 1403 did not have any provisions providing an indigent defendant with an inquiry into his/her ability to pay, this did not mean that § 1403 was constitutionally infirm per se. Pa. R. Crim. P. 706 was an adequate procedural tool that supplanted 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1403, and satisfied the constitutional requirements of Fuller, by ensuring that an indigent defendant was afforded an opportunity to prove his financial inability to pay the costs of prosecution before being sentenced to prison. Therefore, § 1403 was constitutional on its face and as applied to the particular facts of the instant case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

623 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Commonwealth v. Kriner


No. 529 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2007 PA Super 3; 915 A.2d 653; 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1


September 6, 2006, Argued  
January 2, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Jefferson County, Criminal Division, No. CP-33-CR-281-2003. Before FORADORA, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, and indecent assault. Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Jefferson County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: 42 Pa.C.S. § 5985.1(a.1) required that in order to make a finding under 42 Pa.C.S. § 5985.1(a)(2)(ii), the trial court had to determine, based on evidence presented to it, that testimony by the child as a witness would have resulted in the child suffering serious emotional distress that would have substantially impaired the child's ability to reasonably communicate. The victim's death in a car accident prevented the trial court from making a determination under 42 Pa.C.S. § 5985.1(a.1). Since that prerequisite to a determination of unavailability could not be made, 42 Pa.C.S. § 5985.1 did not apply, and the court was constrained to conclude that the trial court erred in its application and in the subsequent admission of the hearsay statements in violation of the confrontation clause. The Pa. R. Evid. 804 definition of unavailable did not apply since the General Assembly crafted a separate and distinct definition of unavailable when the circumstances warranted the application of 42 Pa.C.S. § 5985.1.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's judgment of sentence and remanded.

SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to 9 to 18 months' imprisonment after it convicted him of unlawful contact with a minor, a violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 6318.


OVERVIEW: When the victim was fourteen, she joined an internet chat room which required all participants to be at least eighteen. Nissley v. Candytown Motorcycle Club, Inc.


No. 1772 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 349; 913 A.2d 887; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4472


August 29, 2006, Argued  
December 6, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Civil Division, No(s). CI-04-05120. Before GEORGELIS, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant club member sued appellees, a motorcycle club and its vice president, alleging their negligence caused him to be injured in a motorcycle accident. The Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, Civil Division, found that the claim was barred by an exculpatory clause in the club's membership application and granted appellees summary judgment. The member appealed.


OVERVIEW: While at the club's track, the member rode off of a jump and collided with a tractor driven by the vice president, who was engaged in maintenance work on the track. The trial court held that the exculpatory clause in the club's membership application included unambiguous language that was sufficiently broad to cover the member's negligence claim, and that he assumed the risk of injury since he was aware of the maintenance work but continued riding. The member argued that the exculpatory clause was ambiguous and the trial court should have heard extrinsic evidence in order to determine its scope. The appellate court disagreed. There was no ambiguity in such a clear statement as "I hereby give up all my rights to sue or make claim." A reasonable person would have understood that he was waiving all rights to bring a claim, without qualification. As the release was broader in scope than the assumption of the risk doctrine, the trial court erred in deciding that the doctrine applied.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Cesar

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Ohio), after a jury trial convicting him of indecent assault, corruption of minors, endangering the welfare of a child, and indecent exposure. Commonwealth v. Spease


No. 741 MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 323; 911 A.2d 952; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3775


September 25, 2006, Submitted  
November 13, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Spease, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2237 (Pa., Oct. 22, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Perry County, Criminal Division, No(s): CP-50-CR-0000390-2004. Before REHKAMP, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Perry County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after a nonjury trial in which the trial court convicted defendant of driving under the influence (DUI) of alcohol or a controlled substance, in violation of 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(c). Defendant received a sentence of 90 days to 18 months, a fine, and an 18-month license suspension.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised various constitutional challenges to a new DUI law, 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802 et seq. The court found that it discretion under Pa. R. Crim. P. 575 to rule on the challenges. On review, the court found that the law was valid and upheld the judgment of sentence. The court determined that the rational basis test was applicable to defendant's due process and equal protection challenges because driving was a privilege and not a fundamental right and there was no right to the consumption of any quantity of alcohol before driving. 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(c) was not overbroad because the pertinent question was not what an individual's blood alcohol content (BAC) was at the time of driving but rather what the BAC was as determined by a test taken within two hours of driving. Further, defendant had no standing to challenge portions of the statute other than that provision under which she was convicted. The law did not permit and promote arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement because it was constitutionally permissible to require a person to monitor his or her conduct so as to ensure that he or she did not have a prohibited BAC at any time within two hours of driving.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's judgment of sentence.

Commonwealth v. Hitner


No. 25 EDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 299; 910 A.2d 721; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3540


July 24, 2006, Submitted  
October 27, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Hitner, 592 Pa. 772, 926 A.2d 441, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1080 (May 22, 2007)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Hitner, 11 A.3d 1021, 2010 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4070 (Pa. Super. Ct., Aug. 11, 2010)
Related proceeding at, Application granted by, Writ of mandamus denied Hitner v. Common Pleas Court, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 82 (Pa., Jan. 14, 2013)
Related proceeding at, Writ of mandamus denied, Application granted by Hitner v. Fisher, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 1380 (Pa., June 28, 2013)
Related proceeding at Hitner v. Bucks Cnty. Clerk of Courts, 112 A.3d 701, 2015 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 255 (Apr. 10, 2015)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Hitner, 122 A.3d 1119, 2015 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1580 (June 2, 2015)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Hitner, 2016 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 3092 (Nov. 18, 2016)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Hitner, 2017 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1890 (May 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: Commonwealth v. Hitner, 2006 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 765 (Feb. 24, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant for two counts each of rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, kidnapping, sexual assault, and false imprisonment. The trial court also found defendant was a sexually violent predator, pursuant to Pennsylvania Megan's Law III, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9791-9799.8.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from the sexual assault of two women. Upon executing a search warrant of defendant's home, the police found hair from the victims and semen recovered from them matched defendant as well. The court found that during the Megan's Law III hearing, a doctor testified that defendant had an antisocial personality disorder characterized by a lifetime history of failure to conform his conduct to the norms of society, a callous disregard for other's feelings, and no remorse for his crimes. The court found that the Commonwealth proved by clear and convincing evidence that defendant suffered from a mental abnormality and/or personality disorder based on the extreme nature of the sadism in the case and the fact there was a separation of time between when defendant picked up the two victims. The court found no merit to defendant's sentencing challenges and that the jury's verdict was not against the weight of the evidence. The court noted that the alleged inconsistencies defendant pointed out between the two victims were minor in light of the overwhelming evidence presented against him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.

Commonwealth v. Segida


No. 2222 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 296; 912 A.2d 841; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3534


June 26, 2006, Submitted  
October 24, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Segida, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 10 (Pa. Super. Ct., Jan. 4, 2007)
Appeal granted by Commonwealth v. Segida, 594 Pa. 524, 937 A.2d 419, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2504 (2007)
Vacated by, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Segida, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2785 (Pa., Dec. 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, No. CC 2004-16500. Before ALLEN, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a bench trial in the Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), of two counts of driving under the influence (DUI), in violation of 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(a)(1) and (c), and one count of careless driving. He appealed, alleging the evidence was insufficient and that § 3802(c) was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: An officer saw defendant and his vehicle in brush by the side of a road. Defendant said he lost control of his vehicle. The officer detected a strong odor of alcohol, administered field sobriety tests, and arrested defendant after he failed to complete them to the officer's satisfaction. His blood alcohol content (BAC) was .326 percent. The appellate court held that given the broad wording of defendant's Pa.R.A.P. 1925 statement, he failed to preserve his constitutional challenges to the DUI statute. But it agreed with his contention that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his convictions for both 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802(a)(1) and (c). The § 3802(c) conviction could not stand, as an element of that offense was having a BAC of .16 percent or more within two hours of driving, and there was no evidence as to when his blood was drawn. His § 3802(a)(1) conviction was also invalid, as there was no evidence as to the time of his drinking or when he last drove. Though the officer may have promptly responded to a dispatch reporting the accident, there was no evidence as to when the accident occurred; moreover, no circumstances negated the possibility of post-accident alcohol consumption.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence for DUI was reversed and the judgment of sentence for careless driving was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Woods


No. 1600 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 275; 909 A.2d 372; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3055


April 10, 2006, Submitted  
October 2, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Woods, 591 Pa. 714, 919 A.2d 957, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 553 (2007)
Subsequent appeal at Commonwealth v. Woods, 2009 PA Super 19, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 27 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No. 04-08-1025. Before Dembe, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered after a no contest plea in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) for corruption of minors and aggravated indecent assault. Defendant was found to be a sexually violent predator (SVP). Defendant appealed, filed a timely Pa.R.A.P. 1925(b) statement, and filed an untimely supplemental Rule 1925(b) statement. The trial court addressed both Rule 1925(b) statements.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the Pennsylvania Megan's Law II, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9791 et seq., was unconstitutional. The appellate court held that as defendant did not file a separate petition seeking prior court approval before filing his untimely supplemental Pa.R.A.P. 1925(b) statement, he waived the specific challenges to Megan's Law he set forth in that statement. Defendant generally claimed that Megan's Law II was unconstitutional in his initial Rule 1925(b) statement and in his Pa. R. Crim. P. 704(B) motion, but as defendant did not file a timely post-sentence motion, he failed to preserve any general challenge to the constitutionality of Megan's Law II. There was sufficient evidence to find that defendant was an SVP. A psychologist's failure to interview defendant and his reliance on defendant's juvenile behavior did not preclude the SVP finding. Defendant was 21 when he committed the offenses and had been almost continuously committed to mental health and juvenile detention facilities throughout his teenage years. His inappropriate behavior included repeated episodes of sexually inappropriate or assaultive behavior. There was sufficient evidence that defendant was an SVP.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Merolla


No. 2243 EDA 2005, No. 2232 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 269; 909 A.2d 337; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3049


April 5, 2006, Argued  
September 28, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence and the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Criminal Division, No. 1235 Criminal 2003; 1318 Criminal 2003; 898 Criminal 2003. Before MILLER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court consolidated the parties' appeals with regard to the Commonwealth technically appealing from defendant's judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania) to modify the same. Defendant appealed a sua sponte order that increased his term of registration with police to lifetime, though the trial court did not find defendant was a sexually violent predator (SVP).


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth claimed that the trial court erred in not designating defendant an SVP under Megan's Law II, contending that its burden of proof was satisfied by an expert's testimony identifying defendant as an SVP, citing that expert's conclusion that defendant met the criteria for SVP status because he suffered from mental abnormalities, specifically pedophilia and paraphilia. As such, the Commonwealth argued that the trial court should give defendant a longer period of incarceration, find him to be a SVP, and subject him to lifetime registration under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.1(b)(1). The court disagreed and held that the Commonwealth did not meet its burden of proving defendant was a SVP since the Commonwealth did not provide clear and convincing evidence demonstrating that defendant was likely to re-offend because he suffered from a mental defect or personality disorder. The court found the Commonwealth's expert testified that defendant did not satisfy several of the criteria in 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.4 supporting SVP classification.


OUTCOME: The court vacated both orders and remanded the case to the trial court for the trial court to impose terms of probation.

Commonwealth v. Johnson


NO. 3155 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 265; 910 A.2d 60; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3012


May 18, 2006, Argued  
September 26, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Johnson, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5603 (Pa. Super. Ct., Nov. 30, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Johnson, 592 Pa. 766, 923 A.2d 1173, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1041 (2007)
Appeal after remand at Commonwealth v. Johnson, 2008 PA Super 269, 2008 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3927 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Criminal Division, No. 735-03. Before OSBORNE, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment entered in the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), sentencing him to serve an aggregate term of imprisonment from 22 years to 49 years, imposed after a jury found him guilty of attempted murder, aggravated assault, recklessly endangering another person, intimidation of a witness, retaliation against a witness, and unlawful possession of a firearm. Defendant's post-sentence motions were denied.


OVERVIEW: Three weeks after the victim testified as a witness in a murder prosecution against defendant's brother, two men, one of whom the victim and her sister immediately recognized as defendant, shot at her. Both women separately identified defendant's photograph. The court vacated the sentence. Defendant's motion for a new trial based on the weight of the identification evidence was properly denied because the identifications were reliable. His challenge to the sufficiency of the evidence failed because it was based on witness credibility, which was within the province of the factfinder. Evidence that defendant fled at the time of his arrest was properly admitted. The sentence of 17 and 1/2 to 40 years imposed on defendant for attempted murder was illegal because no sentence greater than 20 years could be imposed under 18 Pa.C.S. § 1102(c) when the jury was not asked to determine whether serious bodily injury resulted. Defendant was not charged with attempted murder resulting in serious bodily injury. His conviction for aggravated assault did not supply the required finding because it was solely the jury's responsibility to find each element of each offense beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The appellate court vacated the entire sentence imposed upon defendant and otherwise affirmed the judgment. The matter was remanded to the trial court for a new sentencing hearing and for resentencing.

Commonwealth v. Leddington


No. 122 EDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 250; 908 A.2d 328; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2990


July 24, 2006, Submitted  
September 12, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Leddington, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2731 (Pa., Dec. 20, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division, No. 6221/2001. Before GOLDBERG, J.
Commonwealth v. Leddington, 2005 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 387, 75 Pa. D. & C.4th 294 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to indecent assault and corruption of minors. The Commonwealth petitioned for a determination that defendant was a sexually violent predator (SVP) under the Pennsylvania Megan's Law II, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9791-- 9799.7. The Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, granted the petition. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant admitted that he sexually assaulted an 11-year-old girl. At the time of the assault, he was on parole and being treated for sexually assaulting his eight-year-old niece over a three-year period. The appellate court held that as the medical evidence showed that defendant was diagnosed with pedophilia, and that this condition could not be cured, he could not show that the lack of judicial reviewability of an SVP finding in Megan's Law II rendered it unconstitutionally overbroad and excessive. His "void for vagueness" challenge also failed. First, the term "sexually violent predator" as used in 42 Pa.C.S. § 9792, and the lesser included words and phrases contained therein, were not unconstitutionally vague. Second, defendant failed to present evidence that the criteria enumerated in 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.4(b), which were used to determine SVP status, were non-predictive. Finally, evidence that he repeatedly committed sexual assaults upon two young girls, and assaulted the second girl while he was under probationary supervision and was receiving counseling for a prior assault on the first girl, was sufficient to establish that he was an SVP.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

In the Interest of J.E.


No. 1042 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 249; 907 A.2d 1114; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2635


January 3, 2006, Submitted  
September 8, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal granted by In the Interest of J.E., 926 A.2d 974, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1249 (Pa., 2007)
Affirmed by In the Interest of J.E., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2892 (Pa., Dec. 27, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Civil Division (Pennsylvania), entered a commitment order confining a minor, a juvenile, to Youth Forestry Camp following a finding that he had committed delinquent acts and violated his probation. The minor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court adjudicated the minor delinquent for possession of a firearm after a probation officer found a gun under a mattress where the minor was sitting. Officers went to the home to serve an arrest warrant on the minor's brother. The minor's stepmother told the officers that the brother was not home but that the minor was in his bedroom. Officers entered the bedroom and found the gun. The appellate court found that, as a proper protective sweep was based on an initial arrest, and as the officers had not arrested the brother, the search of the home for the brother did not implicate the protective sweep doctrine. The fact the minor was shaking during the pat-down search, which showed the officer that the minor was hiding something, was irrelevant since the threshold issue was if the officer had reasonable suspicion of criminal activity or a probation violation before the personal search. A tip that the minor was involved in a shooting did not form reasonable suspicion because the officer could not articulate whether this information was from a known or anonymous informant, and had no specific and articulable facts which reasonably suggested that this tip was correct.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed and the case was remanded.

Commonwealth v. Kearns


No. 1012 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 244; 907 A.2d 649; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2266


December 5, 2005, Submitted  
August 31, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As corrected September 5, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Criminal Division, No. CP-20-CR-0000793-2004. Before MILLER, J.
Commonwealth v. Kearns, 2006 PA Super 75, 896 A.2d 640, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 300 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant after a jury trial of involuntary manslaughter, aggravated assault, endangering the welfare of children, escape, and resisting arrest. As to the charge of involuntary manslaughter, a sentence of six to 12 years' imprisonment, graded as a felony of the second degree, was imposed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from the death of his girlfriend's nearly three year-old daughter. On appeal, defendant contended that sentencing him in excess of five years' imprisonment on the involuntary manslaughter conviction violated his Fifth Amendment right to trial by jury as set forth in Apprendi in that Apprendi required a jury to find the facts that under the involuntary manslaughter statute elevating the grading of that offense from a misdemeanor of the first degree to a felony of the second degree. The trial court imposed that sentence despite the fact that the jury was not charged that in order to find defendant guilty of involuntary manslaughter in the second degree, they must find that the victim was under 12 years of age and also in the care, custody, or control of the person who caused the death. The court found the failure of the trial court to submit those factual determinations to be error. The court held that those factual determinations constituted elements of the offense of involuntary manslaughter graded as a felony of the second degree and must either be admitted by defendant or found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence imposed on the involuntary manslaughter charge and remand the case to the trial court for resentencing. The court directed that defendant must be sentenced on involuntary manslaughter graded as a misdemeanor of the first degree.

Commonwealth v. Askew


No. 697 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 239; 907 A.2d 624; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2227


May 30, 2006, Submitted  
August 30, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Askew, 591 Pa. 709, 919 A.2d 954, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 544 (Pa., Mar. 12, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No. CP#0212-1256. Before GEROFF, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pled nolo contendere to several offenses after he molested a three-year-old girl. Prior to a Megan's Law II, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9791-99, hearing, appellant's motion for extraordinary relief was denied. The Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) found appellant to be a sexually violent predator, imposed sentence, and ordered him to comply with the Megan's Law II registration requirements. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court concluded that appellant's constitutional claims were waived because Pa. R. Crim. P. 704(B)(3) specifically declared that any motion for extraordinary relief had to be preserved via a post-trial motion. Appellant's pre-sentence motion alone was neither necessary nor sufficient to preserve the issues for appeal. Moreover, appellant's constitutional claims merely required the appellate court to revisit issues that had already been decided; the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania had previously held that the requirements of Megan's Law II did not constitute punishment. Also, the evidence was sufficient to prove appellant was a sexually violent predator. The Commonwealth established that he had been convicted of a sexually violent offense as set forth in 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.1; specifically, appellant pled nolo contendere to aggravated indecent assault and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. Then, the Commonwealth established, through the testimony of an expert who appellant stipulated was an expert in the field of sexual offender evaluation and treatment, that appellant had a personality disorder that made him likely to engage in predatory sexually violent offenses.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.
