Commonwealth v. Dixon


NO. 31 MDA 2006


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 221; 907 A.2d 533; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2160


May 15, 2006, Submitted  
August 16, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As corrected August 18, 2006. As corrected August 22, 2006.
Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Dixon, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3494 (Pa. Super. Ct., Oct. 17, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Dixon, 591 Pa. 722, 920 A.2d 830, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 724 (Pa., Mar. 27, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered, November 30, 2005 in the Court of Common Pleas of LANCASTER County, Criminal Division, No. 5855-2002. Before ALLISON, J.
Commonwealth v. Dixon, 578 Pa. 569, 854 A.2d 478, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 1638 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted, upon a guilty plea, of aggravated indecent assault, indecent assault, corruption of minors, and terroristic threats. The Lancaster County Common Pleas Court, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), directed defendant to submit to the non-sexually violent predator (SVP) offender provisions of Megan's Law II, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9791-99.9. Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence, which on remand classified him as an SVP.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth argued that its expert's testimony supported the SVP finding. The appellate court held that the expert testified that defendant suffered from paraphelia NOS, based on defendant's criminal history, which included convictions for the sexual assault of a 14-year-old girl and the rape of a woman. Defendant did not challenge the diagnosis. The expert's ultimate opinion regarding defendant's SVP status did not rest solely on the diagnosis of a mental abnormality. The expert testified as to the statutory factors set out in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9795.4(b) and applied the factors to defendant's case. The factors that weighed toward an SVP classification included the number of victims, the nature of the contact (penetration), the age of the victims, defendant's criminal history, defendant's history of alcohol abuse, and defendant's use of a shotgun to threaten one of the victims. The expert properly concluded that defendant was likely to re-offend, based on defendant's mental abnormality, defendant's prior history of sexual offenses, and the fact that despite serving time in prison for the prior convictions, defendant committed the instant crimes.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Jackson


No. 2922 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 218; 907 A.2d 540; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2159


April 4, 2006, Argued  
August 16, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Jackson, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3493 (Pa. Super. Ct., Oct. 12, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Jackson, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1907 (Pa., Sept. 13, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No.: 04-07-0380. Before FLEISHER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a bench trial in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) of simple assault, possession of a controlled substance, and resisting arrest, following the denial of his pre-trial motion to suppress evidence. He was sentenced to 18 months of reporting probation. Defendant appealed and complied with a court order to file a Pa. R. App. P. 1925(b) statement.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that there was not probable cause to support an investigatory stop. The officers found a drug suspect with a group of men in an area known for drug problems, gun problems, and violent reactions to the police. Under the totality of the circumstances, there was a reasonable suspicion that the drug suspect's companions were armed and dangerous. A safety-oriented frisk was warranted. The police properly ordered defendant to place his hands on a fence and to submit to a frisk. Although defendant at first complied, he then began pushing off the fence. Defendant kicked backward, striking the officer in the knee, which resulted in bruising and swelling. Thus, there was sufficient evidence to support the simple assault conviction. The assault provided grounds for defendant's arrest. Defendant struggled during the attempt to arrest him. He was taken into custody, but continued to resist attempts to subdue him by spitting blood and saliva at the officer, which created a substantial risk of bodily injury. The evidence supported the resisting arrest conviction. Finally, the search of defendant incident to the assault arrest was valid. The suppression motion was properly denied.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Mullins


No. 660 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 215; 905 A.2d 1009; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2131


March 22, 2006, Submitted  
August 10, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Mullins, 594 Pa. 712, 937 A.2d 444, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2577 (2007)
Remanded by Commonwealth v. Mullins, 976 A.2d 1211, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2971 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Mullins v. Rozum, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 150549 (E.D. Pa., Nov. 14, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division, No(s): 4098/2003. Before GOLDBERG, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of multiple sexual offenses and imposed sentences thereon. The trial court also determined that defendant was a sexually violent predator (SVP) pursuant to Megan's Law II, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9791-9799.9 (as amended in 2000).


OVERVIEW: The charges against defendant arose from his sexual abuse of his stepdaughter, who was less than 13 years old at the time that the crimes were committed. His convictions were accepted following his entry of a guilty plea, and defendant was classified as an SVP following a hearing pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9795.4(e)(1). In a post-sentence hearing, defendant's constitutional challenges to Megan's Law II and his claim that there was insufficient evidence to support the SVP determination were denied. On appeal, the court found no merit to defendant's constitutional challenges. After an examination of the two-level analysis of Mendoza-Martinez adopted in Williams II, the court held that the lack of a mechanism for judicial review of SVP status did not render Megan's Law II unconstitutionally excessive. Defendant had not established that he had a curable personality disorder. Further, his void-for-vagueness challenge also failed, as he did not show that the assessment procedures for a determination of SVP classification were unreliable. There was clear and convincing evidence that supported the SVP determination concerning defendant, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9792.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Commonwealth v. Thur


No. 1763 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 208; 906 A.2d 552; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2098


May 24, 2006, Argued  
August 4, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Thur, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 426 (Pa., Apr. 9, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 1129 CRIM 2004. MARTIN, J., JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after defendant's conviction for involuntary manslaughter, homicide by vehicle while driving under the influence (DUI), homicide by vehicle (non-DUI related), driving under the influence, and numerous summary traffic violations.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was observed by another driver striking an on-coming truck head-on with his car, killing the truck's two occupants. Upon arrival at the scene, the police found opened and unopened cans of beer. Defendant's Blood Alcohol Content (BAC) was tested and he was found intoxicated, in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3802. On appeal, defendant contested the constitutionality of the main statutes he was convicted under, particularly because the two hour time frame provided in § 3802(c) allegedly violated due process as a driver was unable to gauge when his or her drinking ripened into a crime. The court found § 3802(c) was neither vague nor overly broad. The court noted that defendant wrongly assumed that one of the essential elements of the offense was having a 0.16 percent BAC at the time of driving, but a person's blood alcohol level at driving time simply was not part of § 3802(c). The court did determine that the DUI and homicide by vehicle while DUI merged for sentencing purposes and that, therefore, defendant was entitled to have the DUI sentence vacated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and vacated the judgment of sentence on the DUI counts only.

Commonwealth v. Reeves


No. 1470 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 196; 907 A.2d 1; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1683


June 20, 2006, Submitted  
July 31, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Revised October 10, 2006.
Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Reeves, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3183 (Pa. Super. Ct., Sept. 29, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Reeves, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 550 (Pa., Mar. 13, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of May 19, 2005 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Nos. 0305-0883 1/1. GEROFF, JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after defendant was convicted of securing execution of documents by deception in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1440.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly induced his attorney to file a claim with the Southeastern Pennsylvania Transit Authority (SEPTA), asserting he sustained injuries on a SEPTA trolley when it suddenly stopped. In fact, there were no passengers on the trolley when the power became disconnected. Defendant's Pa. R. App. P. 1925(b) statement stated only that the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict on the charge of securing execution of documents by deception. Because of that general language, the trial judge only discussed the sufficiency of the evidence in general terms. A concise statement that was too vague to allow the trial court to identify the issues raised on appeal constituted a waiver of those issues. Defendant's claim on appeal was that SEPTA was not a "person" under the terms of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1440. Because the specific issue as to whether SEPTA was a "person" under § 1440 was not presented to the trial court, the issue was waived.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

In the Interest of S.J.


No. 1593 WDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 195; 906 A.2d 547; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1680


February 22, 2005, Submitted  
July 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended May 1, 2007.
Reargument denied by In the Interest of S.J., 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3174 (Pa. Super. Ct., Sept. 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County. Juvenile Division, No. 1921-02. Before WARD, J.
In re S.J., 586 Pa. 461, 894 A.2d 1288, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 502 (Pa., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the state supreme court, appellant county child welfare agency appealed an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) to provide financial assistance to an 18-year-old college student who had been residing in foster care.


OVERVIEW: Since her adjudication of dependency, the student had been in the care of her paternal aunt and uncle. Prior to her 18th birthday, the student signed an affidavit requesting that she remain under the care and supervision of the agency. Thereafter, the student graduated from high school and received a college scholarship. The trial court ordered the agency to provide the student with a college rate of $ 14 per day while she was living away from her foster home and enrolled in college classes. On remand, the appellate court found that the decision in Curtis, finding that Act 62, 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4327(a), was unconstitutional, had no application because Act 62 only pertained to private support matters. Moreover, the case was not about the agency providing post-secondary educational support. Accordingly, the reasoning underlying the prior decision remained applicable and the agency had to provide financial assistance to the student.


OUTCOME: The judgment was re-affirmed.

 

645 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Wilkins v. Marsico


No. 1775 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 188; 903 A.2d 1281; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1647


March 13, 2006, Submitted  
July 21, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Wilkins v. Marsico, 591 Pa. 704, 918 A.2d 747, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 458 (Feb. 28, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Civil Division, No. 1734 CV 2004. Before HOOVER, J.
Commonwealth v. Wilkins, 897 A.2d 524, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 168 (Pa. Super. Ct., Feb. 13, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner filed a complaint against appellee county district attorney (DA) charging misconduct under 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1405. A series of evidentiary motions in limine and a motion for change of venue were filed and processed. The prisoner appealed an order entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) granting the DA's motion to dismiss. The DA moved to quash the prisoner's brief.


OVERVIEW: The DA did not object to the appellate court's exercise of jurisdiction. The appellate court retained jurisdiction. The prisoner's brief failed to present legal arguments or legal citations supporting the prisoner's claims, but the claims were reviewed. The prisoner was primarily concerned with inconsistencies between the victim's testimony at the prisoner's criminal trial and a medical report. However, the differences between the victim's testimony and the medical report were fully explored during the criminal trial and the jury resolved the discrepancies and made credibility determinations. Even if the actions of the deputy district attorney (DDA) who prosecuted the prisoner and the DDA who approved the charges were faulty, the prisoner failed to implicate the DA. As § 1405 provided for criminal penalties, liability had to be based on personal causation; imposition of liability pursuant to a respondeat superior theory was impermissible. The prisoner did not support his allegations of wrongdoing or willful and gross negligence by the DA. Finally, the prisoner admitted that he filed the suit due to the filing of an Anders brief in his criminal proceedings.


OUTCOME: The DA's motion to quash the prisoner's brief was denied as moot. The order dismissing the case was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Little


No. 363 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 186; 903 A.2d 1269; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1643


March 15, 2006, Argued  
July 20, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division, No. 4428 CA 2004. Before CHRONISTER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which was imposed after defendant was convicted of driving under the influence (DUI) and driving under suspension, DUI-related, in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3802(b) and 1543(b)(1).


OVERVIEW: A police officer heard a racing engine and saw defendant's vehicle speed past while continuing to accelerate on an uphill street. The officer intended to give defendant a citation or a warning for driving at an unsafe speed in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3361. However, based on the officer's observations of defendant's slurred speech, uncoordinated movements, his unwillingness to speak directly to him, and an open case of beer in the back seat, as well as defendant's failed field sobriety test and his blood alcohol test results, it was determined that defendant was intoxicated and that his driver's license was suspended. Defendant's suppression motion was denied on the basis that the amendment to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308(b) rendered the standard of Whitmyer inapplicable. Defendant was convicted as charged and he appealed. The court found that the Whitmyer probable cause standard was no longer applicable due to the amendment of § 6308(b), which became effective prior to defendant's conduct. "Reasonable suspicion" under that standard was met. A challenge to the constitutionality of § 6308(b) was not preserved for review under Pa. R. App. P. 302(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

647 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Lamm v. Fisher


No. 2028 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 185; 903 A.2d 1259; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1641


May 15, 2006, Submitted  
July 19, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division, No. 00-011849. Before HORGOS, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division (Pennsylvania), denied a petition pursuant to 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7293(a)-(c) by appellant, an owner of real estate, for redemption of property which had been purchased by appellee, a purchaser, at a sheriff's sale. The owner appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The owner argued that the trial court erred in denying the petition for redemption pursuant to the Municipal Claims and Tax Liens Law, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7293. The appellate court found that the ability of a prior owner to redeem property sold at a sheriff's sale contained a restriction outlined in 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7293(c), which limited redemption to non-vacant property occupied as a residence. The property in question was a mixed use property in that a restaurant and tavern were located on the lower floor, with the upper floors being residential in nature. The owner acknowledged that the upper floors were not occupied at the time in question. Therefore, the appellate court held that the owner did not have a statutory right to redeem the property in question. The appellate court also held that the owner failed to raise an argument that 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7239 was unconstitutional during the trial. Therefore, that argument could not be considered on appeal. Thus, the appellate court held that the trial court properly denied the owner's petition for redemption.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

 

648 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Cordoba


No. 1747 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 165; 902 A.2d 1280; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1578


May 8, 2006, Submitted  
July 7, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Criminal Division, No. CP-06-CR-586-2005. Before BUCCI, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Commonwealth's complaint against defendant for reckless endangerment was dismissed on a motion for habeas corpus relief, the Commonwealth dismissed its appeal and re-filed the action. The Commonwealth appealed the dismissal, upon defendant's motion for habeas corpus relief, by the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), of the re-filed complaint against defendant for reckless endangerment.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth argued that it was permissible to re-file the complaint. The appellate court held that the procedure employed by the Commonwealth did not violate defendant's procedural due process rights. The Commonwealth established a prima facie case against defendant. It was a misnomer to say that the sex between defendant and the victim was consensual as defendant did not inform the victim of his HIV infection. If conduct by an HIV-positive defendant satisfied the elements of recklessly endangering another person, the Commonwealth could prosecute that defendant. The Commonwealth established that defendant knew that he was infected with HIV when he had sex with the victim. Judicial notice should have been taken that defendant's conduct might have placed the victim in danger of serious bodily injury as the conduct might have placed the victim in danger of contracting HIV. Finally, the Commonwealth presented sufficient evidence to make a prima facie case that defendant's decision to have oral sex with the victim without informing him that defendant was HIV-positive was a gross deviation from the standard of conduct that a reasonable person would observe.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Commonwealth v. Semuta


No. 1518 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 163; 902 A.2d 1254; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1546


May 23, 2006, Submitted  
June 30, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Semuta, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2053 (Pa., Sept. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Criminal Division, No. CP-21-CR-2761-2004. Before BAYLEY, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), after he was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol, high rate. Defendant argued, in part, that denial of both his omnibus pretrial motion and a motion in limine was error.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped after he was observed driving a vehicle after dark without the headlights on. Finding no error in the denial of the two motions, the court affirmed, holding that 1) the arresting officer's observations of defendant's vehicle and defendant's condition after the traffic stop provided the necessary reasonable suspicion for administering a 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(k) breath test; 2) coupled with the results of that test, those factors provided probable cause for defendant's arrest under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3802; 3) § 3802 was neither vague nor overbroad and it did not encourage arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement; 5) as defendant had not been convicted under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3804, he could not argue that the statute was vague or that its application violated due process; 6) there was no Sixth Amendment right to have counsel when deciding whether to submit to a blood alcohol content (BAC) test; and 7) a BAC test could be administered without obtaining a search warrant.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Charlton


No. 41 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 149; 902 A.2d 554; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1492


March 13, 2006, Submitted  
June 23, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Charlton, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2131 (Pa., Nov. 8, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Criminal Division, No(s): 3513-03. Before PARISI, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, incest, endangering the welfare of a child, corruption of minors, indecent exposure, and indecent assault. Defendant was sentenced by the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) as a sexually violent predator (SVP) to 10 years and three months to 32 years of imprisonment. Defendant's post-sentence motions were denied and he appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the victim was improperly allowed to testify by closed circuit television. The appellate court held that the victim was properly allowed to testify by closed circuit television as there was expert testimony that the victim suffered from depression, suicidal thoughts, and post-traumatic stress disorder, which likely would impact her ability to testify effectively, and that the presence of defendant in the courtroom would impair the victim's ability to reasonably communicate with the jury. The victim's out-of-court statements to a state police trooper and a child services agency employee were properly admitted under the tender years exception to the hearsay rule as defense counsel had had an opportunity to cross-examine the victim. The convictions were not against the manifest weight of the evidence as the victim testified that defendant rubbed his penis against her, touched her vagina, and had sexual intercourse with her on multiple occasions when she was approximately four and one-half years old. Finally, defendant's classification as an SVP was supported by expert testimony that defendant was a pedophile who was likely to engage in predatory sexual offenses.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Forrester v. Hanson


No. 2820 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 137; 901 A.2d 548; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1444


April 6, 2005, Argued  
June 9, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, No. 01122 February Term, 2003. Before SYLVESTER, J.
Forrester v. Michael Hanson & Sea Gull Lighting Sys., 76 Pa. D. & C.4th 347, 2004 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 99 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed suit against appellees, a driver and his employer, seeking damages for personal injuries. Appellees filed a new matter, which invoked the Pennsylvania Comparative Negligence Act. Appellees were permitted to join appellee doctor. The Philadelphia County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) sustained the doctor's objection to venue and transferred the case to the Montgomery County Court of Common Pleas. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that the claim against the doctor was not a "medical professional liability claim." The appellate court held that the joinder complaint did not set forth a medical professional liability claim under the Pennsylvania Medical Care Availability and Reduction of Error Act, 40 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1303.101 et seq., as it did not demand damages for appellees against the doctor. Appellees did not assert a tort or breach of contract claim against the doctor as patients or representatives of patients. Instead, appellees sought judgment for them as against appellant, or a jury ruling on the doctor's liability to appellant if appellant succeeded. Thus, Pa. R. Civ. P. 1006(a.1) did not apply and the transfer was improper. Appellant's claim of error in the late joinder of the doctor failed. The joinder order was a non-appealable interlocutory order as it did not dispose of all claims and all parties and was not expressly defined as a final order by statute. The trial court did not certify the order as immediately appealable and an order granting joinder of a party was not a basis for an interlocutory appeal as of right. The appellate court lacked jurisdiction to consider the issue.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order sustaining the doctor's objection to venue and transferring the matter was reversed. The matter was remanded to the original trial court for further proceedings.

 

652 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Salamone


No. 10 EDA 2005, No. 305 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 81; 897 A.2d 1209; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 577


August 31, 2005, Argued  
April 10, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Salamone, 923 A.2d 1173, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1077 (Pa., May 22, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), following his convictions for risking a catastrophe and recklessly endangering another person in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3302(b), 2705. He also appealed the trial court order, which denied his motion for return of property and directed his forfeiture of $ 34,000 to the Commonwealth.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to sustain a conviction for risking a catastrophe. The appellate court concluded that, while flying an airplane, absent other factors, might not be per se dangerous, defendant flew his plane while intoxicated for four hours in restricted airspace around a heavily trafficked, urban, restricted airport. He flew at unpredictable speeds, altitudes, and directions, in close proximity to other aircraft. In two instances, defendant's plane flew head-on at other aircraft and came close to the cooling towers of a nuclear power plant. Contact between defendant's aircraft and any of these could have caused widespread damage to the populated areas below, a nuclear power disaster, or injury or death to the other pilots and their passengers. Thus, defendant's intoxicated condition and reckless operation of his aircraft fell within the meaning of "other means of causing potentially widespread injury or damage" in 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3302(a). In addition, the Commonwealth provided a sufficient nexus between the airplane and defendant's crimes to justify the characterization of the plane as derivative contraband, subject to forfeiture.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence of the trial court was affirmed. The order of forfeiture was also affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Kearns


No. 1012 WDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 75; 896 A.2d 640; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 300


December 5, 2005, Submitted  
March 31, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Kearns, 2006 PA Super 244, 907 A.2d 649, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2266 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Criminal Division, No. CP-20-CR-0000793-2004. Before MILLER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to an aggregate term of 14 1/4 to 29 years' imprisonment after he was convicted of involuntary manslaughter, aggravated assault, endangering the welfare of children, escape, and resisting arrest. Defendant's counsel filed a petition to withdraw pursuant to Anders and McClendon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's counsel filed a petition stating that he had made a conscientious review of the record and that the appeal was wholly frivolous. The appellate court concluded that, although appointed counsel fulfilled the mechanical requirements for successfully seeking leave to withdraw his representation, it could not agree with the assessment that the appeal was wholly frivolous. The sole issue briefed, that defendant's sentence violated the decision in Apprendi, was an issue that appeared to have at least arguable merit that compelled briefing by an interested advocate as opposed to one seeking to withdraw his representation due to his assessment that the appeal was wholly frivolous. Specifically, a comparison of the elements of endangering the welfare of children found in 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4304 and 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2504, the provision that elevated the grading of involuntary manslaughter, revealed that the two were not identical. In addition, the lack of a complete transcript prevented the appellate court from discharging its obligation to conduct an independent review of the record to determine whether there existed any issues of arguable merit to pursue on appeal.


OUTCOME: The appellate court denied counsel's petition to withdraw and remanded the case for the filing of an advocate's brief and completion of the record.

Commonwealth v. Derk


No. 586 MDA 2005, No. 591 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 60; 895 A.2d 622; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 251


November 29, 2005, Argued  
March 23, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Bingaman, 588 Pa. 768, 905 A.2d 499, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1577 (Pa., Aug. 24, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Derk, 589 Pa. 718, 907 A.2d 1101, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1726 (Pa., Sept. 11, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Snyder County, Criminal Division, Nos. CR 0201-2004 and CR-0287-2004. Before WOELFEL, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Snyder County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) entered judgments in a first case and a second case that ordered the first defendant and the second defendant, respectively, to submit DNA samples to be stored in a DNA data bank and to pay a mandatory cost of $250. The first defendant and the second defendant appealed, and the appeals were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: In separate cases, the trial court ordered the first defendant and the second defendant to submit a DNA sample to a data bank and pay a mandatory cost of $ 250. Those provisions were ordered pursuant to 44 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 2301-2336 (DNA Act). The first defendant was ordered to do so after her offense of retail theft was graded a felony of the third degree pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3929(b) due to two prior retail theft convictions. The second defendant was ordered to do so based on his guilty plea to two misdemeanor retail theft counts. After the cases were consolidated for the purposes of appeal, the appellate court found that the order as to the first defendant had to be affirmed because her offense was within the purview of the DNA Act that directed the taking of DNA samples and payment of the mandatory fee for those people who had committed felony offenses. It also rejected her contention that the DNA Act violated the ex post facto clause of the federal and state constitutions since the directives of the DNA were administrative, not punitive, in nature. Finally, it concluded that the second defendant's two misdemeanors were not within the purview of the DNA Act.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment against the first defendant was affirmed. However, the judgment against the second defendant was reversed and vacated in part to the extent of the second defendant not having to submit a DNA sample or pay the mandatory $250 fee.

Commonwealth v. Pepe


NO. 1442 WDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 49; 897 A.2d 463; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 178


November 30, 2005, Argued  
March 8, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Commonwealth v. Pepe, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 994 (Pa. Super. Ct., May 18, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Pepe, 946 A.2d 686, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 381 (Pa., 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pepe v. Pennsylvania, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5610 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Criminal Division, CP-10-CR-0001176-2002. Before SHAFFER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence imposed by the Butler County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after defendant was convicted of child pornography offenses under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6312(b), (c), (d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant asked the appellate court to determine whether the 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6312 prohibitions against the sexual abuse of children were unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Defendant claimed that § 6312 criminalized the possession of material containing computer-generated depictions of children engaged in prohibited sexual activity as well as depictions of real children involved in prohibited sexual activity. In a previous case, the appellate court had held that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6312(d) applied only to real rather than computer-generated images and that § 6312(d) was not overbroad when applied to possession of real images. In the instant case, the trial court similarly concluded that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6312 required involvement of a real child rather than a computer-generated image. Thus, the trial court required the Commonwealth to demonstrate that the materials that were seized from defendant depicted actual children and not computer-generated images. In the instant case, defendant was making the same argument as the one that the appellate court rejected in previous case law. The appellate court was bound by its prior decision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. McCoy


No. 670 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 33; 895 A.2d 18; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 108


November 30, 2005, Argued  
February 21, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Commonwealth v. McCoy, 597 Pa. 234, 951 A.2d 264, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1008 (2008)
Affirmed by Commonwealth v. McCoy, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1323 (Pa., July 21, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division, No. 1808-CR-2004. Before KLEINFELTER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of two counts of driving under the influence (DUI) in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3802 and one count of violating 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3309(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant lost control of his vehicle and crashed. Police officers observed that defendant smelled of alcohol, had bloodshot eyes, and slurred his speech. Defendant was arrested and transported to a hospital, where a blood test revealed that defendant's blood alcohol content exceeded the limit stated in 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3802(c). The court held that defendant's violations of two subsections of § 3802 were properly merged for sentencing purposes. Defendant's arrest was not invalid because the arresting officer cited a recently repealed statutory section upon his arrest, rather than the newly enacted § 3802. A warrantless arrest on probable cause was proper under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3811. Because defendant consented to the blood test, he was not entitled to relief on the basis that he was not informed of the consequences of refusal under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547. Defendant had no right to counsel before consenting to the test. 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3802 was not vague or overbroad, and its use of a longer period for enhancement did not constitute an ex post facto law. There was no equal protection violation in imposing higher penalties based on higher amounts of blood alcohol.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.

Md. Cas. Co. v. Odyssey Contr. Corp.


No. 1227 WDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 25; 894 A.2d 750; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 64


March 30, 2005, Argued  
February 9, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Md. Cas. v. Odyssey Contr., 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 614 (Pa. Super. Ct., Apr. 17, 2006)
Appeal denied by Md. Cas. Co. v. Odyssey Contr. Corp., 589 Pa. 739, 909 A.2d 1290, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2099 (Pa., 2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Odyssey Contractor v. Md. Casualty Co., 127 S. Ct. 1887, 167 L. Ed. 2d 365, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 3598 (U.S., Mar. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order entered of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Civil Division, No. 2002-2855. Before GILMORE, J.
Md. Cas. Co. v. Odyssey Contr. Corp., 2005 PA Super 257, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2233 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Civil, Pennsylvania, granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of appellee insurer on its breach of contract claim against appellant companies for failure to pay all of the premiums owed for workers' compensation insurance coverage. The companies appealed.


OVERVIEW: The companies argued that the trial court improperly determined that an adequate administrative remedy existed to address their counterclaim that the insurer applied an incorrect final premium rate when charging for workers' compensation insurance coverage. The appellate court noted that the trial judge explained why he reached the conclusion that the companies could have sought the relief they now requested had they pursued a timely claim before the Pennsylvania Compensation Rating Bureau (Bureau). The Bureau had no authority to adjudicate a contracts dispute, but it did have the authority to rule in a dispute over the application of rate classifications in a given case. The companies argued that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 1035.17 was unconstitutional. The appellate court agreed that § 1035.17 did not give notice as to the time period in which a claimant could initiate action; however, the companies did not challenge the constitutionality of the manner in which the pertinent agency rules were promulgated, nor did they raise a claim that the specific agency rules operated to deprive them of their due process rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Ciccola


No. 239 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 23; 894 A.2d 744; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 60


October 5, 2005, Argued  
February 7, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Ciccola, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1064 (Pa. Super. Ct., Apr. 17, 2006)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Ciccola, 916 A.2d 630, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 73 (Pa., Jan. 8, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal Division, No. 2004-1058. Before KISTLER, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which imposed sentence upon defendant after he refused to consent to submit to a blood alcohol content (BAC) test and was convicted of driving under the influence in a bench trial.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3802(a)(2), (b) and (c) were vague and/or overbroad. The appellate court concluded that because defendant was not convicted of violating these provisions, he had no standing to challenge their constitutionality. Defendant also argued that evidence of his refusal to submit to a BAC test should have been suppressed as he was not provided an opportunity to consult with counsel before deciding whether to give consent to undergo a BAC test. The decision to consent to a BAC test had strategic implications. However, such strategic tactical decisions had not been recognized as critical stages of the proceedings so as to invoke the Sixth Amendment right to counsel. A request to submit to a BAC test represented the government's efforts to gather evidence already in existence. Encounters involving the gathering of evidence were not considered "critical proceedings" for purposes of the right to counsel. Thus, the provision of counsel at this stage was not constitutionally mandated and the denial of the right to confer with counsel prior to deciding whether to submit to chemical testing did not render defendant's refusal inadmissible.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. P.L.S.


No. 2128 WDA 2002


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2006 PA Super 20; 894 A.2d 120; 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 46


September 14, 2004, Argued  
February 2, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 534 (Pa. Super. Ct., Apr. 7, 2006)
Appeal denied by, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 588 Pa. 780, 906 A.2d 542, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1703 (2006)
Decision reached on appeal by, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 988 A.2d 730, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5807 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Schaffer v. Cameron, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 69990 (W.D. Pa., Feb. 20, 2012)
Decision reached on appeal by, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 2014 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1841 (2014)
Affirmed by, Motion denied by Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 125 A.3d 446, 2015 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2119 (2015)
Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 2016 Pa. LEXIS 2508 (Pa., Nov. 2, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Jefferson County, Criminal Division, Nos. CR No. 2000-00569. Before FORADORA, J.
Commonwealth v. Schaffer, 2005 PA Super 14, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 29 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Jefferson County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), convicted defendant of rape, attempted rape, attempted involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, statutory sexual assault, indecent assault, corruption of a minor, and endangering the welfare of a child. It was determined that defendant was a sexually violent predator and he was sentenced to a total term of imprisonment of 26 to 52 years. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victim, the child of defendant's girlfriend, testified that the sexual abuse occurred weekly over a two-year period. In sentencing defendant, the trial court considered evidence of other uncharged crimes, alleging sexual abuse of two other children. The superior court found that the evidence linking defendant to those other crimes of sexual abuse stood unrefuted. That evidence sprang from defendant's own mouth and was confirmed by the victims. In addition, the trial court clearly found, based upon defendant's abuse of his victims, that he was a threat to children and needed to be sentenced above the guidelines ranges for the protection of the public. Even if the superior court were to conclude that the uncharged conduct should not have been considered by the trial court, the trial court offered significant other support for sentencing in excess of the guidelines in the victim's case as he sexually abused her nearly weekly for two years, he attempted to justify his actions, and his propensities and the justification that he offered for his behavior made him a poor candidate for rehabilitation. Thus, his sentence was not unreasonable under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9781(c)(3).


OUTCOME: The superior court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Commonwealth v. Lippert


No. 1344 WDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 399; 887 A.2d 1277; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4168


August 1, 2005, Submitted  
December 1, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, No. CC 200203016. Before DeAngelis, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol with a blood alcohol content of .10 percent or more in violation of former 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(4). He was sentenced to 60 days' intermediate punishment with electronic monitoring, as well as a two-year probationary term effective from sentencing. Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant questioned the sufficiency of the evidence supporting his conviction. The appellate court found that the blood alcohol content (BAC) testing was performed within one hour of driving and established defendant's BAC at .105 percent. Given the .105 percent reading and the five percent margin of error, defendant's BAC was exactly .10 percent one hour after driving. The Commonwealth's expert witness admitted that defendant's BAC could have been less than .10 percent when he was driving when she conceded that she was unable to opine that it was rising between 12:30 a.m. and 1:30 a.m. Since defendant's BAC was exactly .10 percent one hour after driving and since the Commonwealth conceded that it could have been rising, the inference of guilt was too weak to establish proof beyond a reasonable doubt. Thus, the evidence was not sufficient to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant's BAC was .10 percent or over when he was driving.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was vacated, and defendant was discharged.

Commonwealth v. Harris


No. 596 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 388; 888 A.2d 862; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4056


September 6, 2005, Submitted  
November 16, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Harris, 593 Pa. 746, 931 A.2d 656, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1775 (Aug. 30, 2007)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Harris, 23 A.3d 572, 2010 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6288 (Pa. Super. Ct., Dec. 8, 2010)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Harris, 2016 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 4386 (Dec. 2, 2016)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Harris v. Mooney, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 102475 (E.D. Pa., June 28, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No(s): C.P.#02-01-0175. Before SHREEVES-JOHNS, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence of the Philadelphia County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), sentencing him under the three strikes law, Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9714, after he was found guilty of robbery. He challenged the constitutionality of a photo identification, the seizure of a toy gun from his car, and the imposition of an enhanced sentence based on a judge's findings.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had apparently robbed a convenience store at gunpoint, but a toy gun, which officers initially mistook for a real gun, was found in his car when officers stopped the car because of the car's, and defendant's, resemblance to descriptions given by victims of another robbery. The court held, first, that the photo array in which the store clerk identified defendant had not been unduly suggestive. It had been assembled using a computer program that located photographs of other persons who resembled defendant, and the motions judge had commented on the quality of the random sample. The toy gun was also properly not suppressed. The officers had a reasonable, articulable suspicion that the car had been involved in another robbery, and, furthermore, the officers were empowered by statute to stop the car once they saw its expired license tag. Once the car was stopped, the toy gun was in plain view. Finally, the exception to Apprendi's requirements for enhancements based solely on criminal history validated the three strikes law as defendant's prior convictions were amply demonstrated on the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

662 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Tustin


No. 156 MDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 386; 888 A.2d 843; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4053


May 16, 2005, Submitted  
November 15, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County, Criminal Division, No(s). 341 CA 2004. Before KURTZ, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered in the Huntingdon County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after his conviction of a second drunk driving offense. He argued that his sentencing as a recidivist violated due process because instead of applying a seven-year lookback period, pursuant to former 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731, the trial court applied the amended 10-year lookback period of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3806.


OVERVIEW: At the time of defendant's first drunk driving arrest, the former statute had provided that successive convictions within seven years would result in sentencing as a second offender. Three months before his second arrest, § 3806 was enacted, changing the lookback period to 10 years. Defendant argued that application of the enhanced sentencing provision to him violated his due process rights because it effectively imposed a greater penalty on him for the original offense. The court held that Pennsylvania and federal law had consistently found no due process violation in a situation such as defendant's, because defendant was always on notice as to what conduct would result in an enhanced punishment. The legislature was always free to provide for enhanced penalties by amending a sentencing statute, as long as potential violators were advised of the penalties they faced.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.

 

663 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sands


No. 494 EDA 2005


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 372; 887 A.2d 261; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3982


September 14, 2005, Argued  
November 2, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Criminal Division, No. 4985-04. Before HALL, J.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas, Chester County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), upon his conviction of driving under the influence and failing to stay in his lane. He challenged the constitutionality of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308(b), the statute that provided the basis for the traffic stop.


OVERVIEW: An officer with experience in observing and arresting drunk drivers noticed defendant's vehicle drifting back and forth, sometimes leaving his lane. After following him for a while, he stopped defendant, fearing he would collide with an upcoming obstacle. At that point, defendant's intoxicated condition became obvious, and he was arrested. An earlier version of § 6308(b), which required an officer to have articulable and reasonable grounds to believe the Vehicle Code was being violated in order to effect a traffic stop, had been held to require probable cause, but the amended version required only a reasonable suspicion. Defendant argued that this lower standard violated U.S. Const. amend. IV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. The court found no constitutional infirmity, at least where the violation in question involved driving under the influence, one of the few offenses for which a stop to determine if a violation was occurring would be helpful. The interest of public safety weighed heavily when balanced against individuals' rights to be left alone, and a traffic stop alone, based on the observations of an experienced officer, was minimally intrusive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Warren v. Folk


No. 1817 MDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 367; 886 A.2d 305; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3971


August 31, 2005, Argued  
October 28, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Civil Division, No. 2004 CV 2081 CV. Before BRATTON, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County in the Civil Division (Pennsylvania), which denied his motion to strike a judgment of non pros in favor of appellee health care providers that was based on the husband's failure to file a certificate of merit required by Pa. R. Civ. P. 1042.3 to support his medical malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: The husband's wife died of cancer, which the husband attributed to silicone breast implants that caused the cancer and to misdiagnosis that ultimately led to her death. The husband initially filed a medical malpractice action in federal court, but that action was transferred to state court. The husband never filed a certificate of merit required by Pa. R. Civ. P. 1042.3. Judgments of non pros were entered for the providers due to the failure to file the certificate. The husband appealed, arguing that, regardless of the fact that the suit was filed after the effective date of Rule 1042.3, requiring a certificate represented a retroactive application of the rule because the acts of misconduct occurred prior to the effective date of the rule. The rule added a procedural requirement for going forward with the suit and the fact that the husband would draw upon, or relate back, to antecedent events did not constitute a retroactive application of the rule. The rule did not in any way alter the legal effects of the husband's alleged acts of misconduct, so the rule did not have retroactive application. Since the husband filed the suit after the effective date of the rule, the rule applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of non pros in favor of the providers.

Commonwealth v. Mitchell


No. 894 MDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 318; 883 A.2d 1096; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3438


March 28, 2005, Submitted  
September 12, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Mitchell, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 509 (Pa., Apr. 19, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County, Criminal Division, No(s). 926 of 2002. Before RUSSELL, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Schuylkill County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of kidnapping, aggravated assault, and other offenses. Defendant also challenged the sentence imposed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant assaulted another man, who he believed had committed a sexual crime upon his step-daughter. He was convicted of several crimes, and the mandatory minimum sentence was imposed under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9712. Defendant then sought review. In affirming, the court determined that the mandatory minimum sentence for the visible possession of a firearm did not violate Blakely. The Pennsylvania Sentencing Guidelines were not mandatory; the mere fact that the mandatory minimum divested the courts of the discretion to impose a lower sentence did not implicate constitutional concerns. Next, the court held that there was sufficient evidence to support the kidnapping conviction under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2901. The evidence showed that the victim was transported a substantial distance to a secluded area for the assault. Further, the trial court did not abuse its discretion by admitting photographs of the assault scene where evidence was found. The photos were authenticated by the persons who located the evidence. Lastly, testimony about the seizure of a weapon from defendant's home was proper authentication, and blood evidence on defendant's clothes was relevant.


OUTCOME: The convictions and sentences were affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Hargraves


No. 2141 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 294; 883 A.2d 616; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2896


May 10, 2005, Argued  
August 16, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Hargraves, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 565 (Pa., Apr. 25, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No. 0309-5218 1/1. Before KEAN, J.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied its petition to transfer defendant's criminal proceeding from the Philadelphia Municipal Court to the Court of Common Pleas so that it could exercise its right to a jury trial. Defendant was arrested and charged with four counts of theft, receiving stolen property, and theft by failure to make required disposition of funds.


OVERVIEW: The charges alleged that defendant, a police officer, acquired approximately $ 1,500 during four separate narcotics investigations and failed to surrender that money to the Police Evidence Custodian. Because the charges were all graded as misdemeanors of the first degree, defendant's case had been scheduled to be heard by a judge in the municipal court pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1123(a)(2). However, since jury trials were not held in municipal court, and the Commonwealth wanted to exercise its right to a jury trial guaranteed by Pa. Const. art. 1, § 6, it petitioned to transfer the case to the court of common pleas. The court found that Pa. R. Crim. P. 1001 only detailed how a defendant could petition to have the case transferred but made no similar provision for the Commonwealth. Since a procedural rule was unable to usurp the Commonwealth's constitutional right, the proper method of effectuating the Commonwealth's right was to petition the court to transfer the case. The absence of a procedural rule was no basis for denying the Commonwealth its constitutional guarantee of a trial by jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

Commonwealth v. Thomas


No. 2276 WDA 2003


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 245; 879 A.2d 246; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1593


April 20, 2005, Argued  
June 30, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Thomas, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 259 (Pa., Feb. 23, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County, Criminal Division, Nos. 2001-1021 & 2001-968. Before KOPRIVA, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County (Pennsylvania), which convicted and sentenced him for simple assault under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2701(a)(1), terroristic threats under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2706(a)(1), recklessly endangering another person under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2705, retaliation against a witness under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4953, and resisting arrest under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5104.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with multiple crimes arising from two incidents where he attacked his girlfriend. He was also charged in federal court on drug charges. At various times, five attorneys were appointed to aid him, and defendant was also granted a motion to proceed pro se. After he was convicted and sentenced, he appealed. He raised a multitude of issues. The court found that his right to a speedy trial under Pa. R. Crim. P. 600 was not violated because he was unavailable for trial while he was in federal custody on the drug charges. He was also not denied his right under U.S. Const. amend. VI and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9, to be present at trial where he demanded that he be removed from the courtroom and was disruptive. His right to counsel was forfeited by a pattern of abusing his appointed counsel and refusing to cooperate with them in his defense. Change of venue was not warranted without proof of pervasive pretrial publicity, and assault charges were properly consolidated. However, because simple assault was a lesser included offense of reckless endangerment, the crimes should have been merged for sentencing, and a remand for resentencing was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions but remanded for resentencing on simple assault and recklessly endangering another person and for resentencing of a fine that was imposed after a determination of defendant's ability to pay.

Commonwealth v. Scott


No. 1852 EDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 224; 878 A.2d 874; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1503


February 15, 2005, Submitted  
June 20, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 21, 2005.
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Scott, 586 Pa. 749, 892 A.2d 823, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 3226 (Pa., Dec. 29, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, No(s): 04-04-0386.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which found him guilty of carrying a firearm without a license and sentenced him to three years' probation. Defendant was stopped for violating a noise ordinance, Philadelphia, Pa., Code & Charter, Traffic Code § 12-1126, and a handgun was found in plain view in his vehicle.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the noise ordinance. The appellate court found that the ordinance provided fair notice as to the prohibited conduct: it was a violation to operate a sound reproduction device inside a vehicle, in the City of Philadelphia, so that it was capable of being heard outside of the vehicle at a distance of greater than 25 feet. The distance standard provided an explicit guideline to those charged with enforcing the ordinance. Because the ordinance provided fair notice as to prohibited conduct, defendant failed to demonstrate that it was unconstitutional on its face. Defendant's applied challenge also failed because the Commonwealth established defendant's clear violation of the ordinance. The appellate court also concluded that the ordinance was not facially overbroad. The ordinance was content neutral because it restricted excess noise regardless of the content of the communications emanating from sound reproduction devices, and the ordinance was narrowly tailored to serve the city's significant interest in stemming loud noise. Thus, the traffic stop was based on probable cause and the subsequent seizure of the handgun was legal.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Berry


No. 565 MDA 2003


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 219; 877 A.2d 479; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1473


September 9, 2004, Argued  
June 13, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Berry, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 92 (Pa., Jan. 11, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the PCRA Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, Criminal Division, No. 138-02 CR. Before SAXTON, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to one count of driving under the influence and one count of driving while operating privileges were suspended or revoked. He received consecutive sentences. Defendant appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which denied his first petition under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9546.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court violated the plea agreement by imposing consecutive sentences and by not allowing defendant to withdraw his plea. On appeal, defendant argued that his failure to file a direct appeal did not operate as a waiver of his sentencing claim because challenges to the legality of a sentence were never waived. The appellate court found that defendant did not move to withdraw his plea, did not file post-sentence motions, and did not file a direct appeal. Instead, he filed a petition under the PCRA, in which he requested that he be allowed to withdraw his guilty pleas. Because defendant failed to raise his claim in the trial court or on direct appeal, the issue was waived unless an exception to the waiver rule applied. However, the appellate court concluded that no exception applied because current case law did not support the proposition that defendant's claim was a non-waivable challenge to the legality of the sentence where defendant did not identify any statutory basis or double jeopardy basis for declaring that his sentence was illegal. Thus, defendant's issue was waived.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Williams

SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

June 9, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Williams, 586 Pa. 770, 895 A.2d 1261, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 415 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, No. 2000-02416. Before CONNELLY, J.
Commonwealth v. Williams, 574 Pa. 487, 832 A.2d 962, 2003 Pa. LEXIS 1746 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Court vacated the trial court's order and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which, on remand from the supreme court and without holding a hearing, ruled that Pennsylvania's Registration of Sexual Offenders Act (Megan's Law II), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9795.1(b)(2). Defendant sought extraordinary relief, arguing that the SVP provisions of Megan's Law II violated the U.S. and Pennsylvania Constitutions. In re Private Crim. Complaint of Wilson


No. 211 WDA 2003


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 211; 879 A.2d 199; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1464


September 9, 2004, Argued  
June 6, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, No. OTN Pending. Before ANTHONY, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted en banc review of an order of the Erie County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) that denied petitioner arrestee approval of his Pa. R. Crim. P. 506 private complaint seeking to compel respondent Commonwealth to prosecute the local police chief for assault, reckless endangerment, and oppression. The Commonwealth argued that the arrestee lacked standing to appeal disapproval of his complaint.


OVERVIEW: The police chief allegedly roughed up the arrestee, injuring him, when detaining him after an incident in which the arrestee, as a prank, shot the chief with a squirt gun. The arrestee presented the prosecutor with a Pa. R. Crim. P. 506 private criminal complaint, which the prosecutor refused to approve. The court held that the arrestee was a person aggrieved by the decision not to prosecute and that he could seek review in the superior court as well as initial review in a common pleas court. The appeal itself, however, was unsuccessful. The standard of review for declination based on legal reasons was de novo, but for a declination based on policy reasons (or for mixed reasons), as in the instant case where the prosecutor cited unlikelihood of conviction and availability of civil remedies as reasons not to prosecute, the standard was abuse of discretion, and in the absence of any showing of bad faith, fraud, or unconstitutionality, the court would not interfere with the prosecutor's exercise of discretion.


OUTCOME: Although the court held that the arrestee had standing to seek review, it nonetheless affirmed the order.

 

672 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Price


No. 1882 MDA 2003


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 200; 876 A.2d 988; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1352


May 10, 2004, Submitted  
May 27, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Price, 890 A.2d 1058, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2854 (Pa., 2005)
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Price, 897 A.2d 1184, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 453 (Pa., 2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Price v. Pennsylvania, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 5580 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Susquehanna County, Criminal Division, at No. 2001-CR-62. Before SEAMANS, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate's only developed claim in his appeal from an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Susquehanna County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), that denied his petition for relief under the Pennsylvania Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9546, was insufficiency of the evidence to support his designation as a sexually violent predator (SVP) under Pennsylvania's Megan's Law II, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9791-9799.


OVERVIEW: Instead of seeking PCRA relief from his designation as an SVP, the inmate filed a post-sentence motion for special relief. The appellate court noted that under Pennsylvania case law, it would have been entitled to treat such a motion as a PCRA motion, if only the inmate had sought review of its denial, but he had not. Instead, much later, he filed an untimely PCRA petition. By that time, the trial court had lost all jurisdiction of the matter, and there was no jurisdiction even to rule on the petition. Nonetheless, the appellate court looked to the merits of the petition as well and held that the inmate had failed to raise a cognizable claim. A claim challenging the collateral consequences of a guilty plea, as Megan's Law II consequences had been held to be, was not a proper subject of a PCRA petition. Furthermore, it appeared from the record that the evidence was ample to support the inmate's SVP designation given that the inmate's expert had not even addressed certain factors required to be addressed in making such a determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

 

673 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


In re Petition to Compel Cooperation with Child Abuse Investigation


No. 398 Middle District Appeal 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 188; 875 A.2d 365; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1309


October 12, 2004, Argued  
May 20, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Susquehanna County, Civil Division,No. 2004-4936 JUV. Before SEAMANS, J.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural parents challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Susquehanna County (Pennsylvania) compelling their cooperation with appellee Susquehanna County Services for Children and Youth (CY) for the scheduling and completion of a "home visit" of their residence following a child abuse referral.


OVERVIEW: The appeals court found jurisdiction to entertain CY's petition under 55 Pa. Code § 3490.73 and that the Child Protective Services Law (CPSL), 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6301 et seq., and specifically 55 Pa. Code § 3490.55(i), mandating a "home visit" at least once during the investigation period, were subject to the limits of existing Fourth Amendment jurisprudence. CY argued that the court's order did not violate the parents' Fourth Amendment rights because a home visit was required by the Department of Public Welfare's regulations and that the Fourth Amendment did not apply where the safety of children was at issue. The appeals court disagreed. The Fourth Amendment and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 applied to the CPSL and the regulations written to implement it. The only relevant facts alleged were that CY had received a Child Line referral for possible medical neglect. This was insufficient to support the court's order compelling the parents to submit to a search of their home. Nor did CY allege exigent circumstances- the court's order giving the parents 10 days to comply indicated that it was not an emergency situation where the child's life was in imminent danger.


OUTCOME: The order was vacated, and jurisdiction was relinquished.

In re Estate of Zambrano


NO. 687 WDA 2004


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


2005 PA Super 154; 875 A.2d 307; 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 916


December 8, 2004, Argued  
April 26, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Orphans' Court Division, No(s): 7139 of 1995. Before MAZUR, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residuary beneficiaries of a decedent's estate sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Orphans' Court Division (Pennsylvania), which denied all exceptions and cross-exceptions to, and adopted and affirmed with certain exceptions, the master's findings and conclusions relative to, inter alia, the apportionment of federal taxes.


OVERVIEW: The decedent died in 1995, and his second wife filed a petition to apportion and pay death taxes. As administratrix, she filed her first and partial account in 2002. The decedent's son and grandson from his first marriage, as the residuary beneficiaries of the estate, filed objections. The orphan's court adopted the findings of an appointed master, who found that the marital property the wife received was not subject to death taxes and attributed federal taxes as being part of the tax apportioned to the residuary estate. The wife filed a supplemental estate tax return claiming deductions for interest that had been paid on unpaid tax obligations. The master levied the estate. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that 20 Pa. Const. Stat. § 3706 was a "typical" apportionment statute that required that the fiduciary to pay the tax in full before seeking apportionment and contribution; however, the court found that the master followed the general rule of estate tax apportionment under 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3702(a) when he apportioned to the residuary beneficiaries the federal estate tax attributable to the decedent's bank account, which was attributed to the residuary estate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order providing for apportionment and contribution by the residuary beneficiaries and ruled that a final determination of the federal estate tax had to be made by the federal tax officials and the federal estate tax, including interest and penalties, had to be paid in full before the beneficiaries could be required to do so. The court affirmed the finding that the bank account was properly apportioned to the beneficiaries.
